<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:279px">
<div class="center" style="font-weight:bold">Rome City Centre</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1111531371">@media all and (max-width:720px){body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output div.mw-graph{min-width:100%!important;max-width:100%;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:visible}}.mw-parser-output .mw-graph-img{width:inherit;height:inherit}</style><div class="mw-graph mw-graph-always mw-graph-nofallback" data-graph-id="e63b2b4b2d92627f8b705e8d3e61a62943e5019e" style="min-width:277px;min-height:260px"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="font-size:75%"><a class="mw-kartographer-maplink" data-lat="41.897" data-lon="12.476" data-mw="interface" data-overlays='["_917244e67f80b8123d365116d22035c352a450bf"]' data-style="osm-intl" data-zoom="14" href="/wiki/Special:Map/14/41.897/12.476/en">[Interactive fullscreen map]</a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:green; color:white;"> </span> Metro station, use fullscreen to show Termini</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:blue; color:white;"> </span> Point of interest</div></div>
</div></div>
<p><b>Rome</b> (<a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>: <i>Roma</i> <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="it-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Italian" title="Help:IPA/Italian">[ˈroːma]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:It-Roma.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e1/It-Roma.ogg" title="It-Roma.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>) is the <a href="/wiki/Capital_city" title="Capital city">capital city</a> of <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>. It is also the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Lazio" title="Lazio">Lazio</a> <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_Italy" title="Regions of Italy">region</a>, the centre of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metropolitan_City_of_Rome" title="Metropolitan City of Rome">Metropolitan City of Rome</a>, and a special <i><a href="/wiki/Comune" title="Comune">comune</a></i> named <i>Comune di Roma Capitale</i>. With 2,860,009 residents in 1,285 km<sup>2</sup> (496.1 sq mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Population_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Population-2">[1]</a></sup> Rome is the country's most populated <i>comune</i> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_the_European_Union_by_population_within_city_limits" title="List of cities in the European Union by population within city limits">third most populous city</a> in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> by population within city limits. The Metropolitan City of Rome, with a population of 4,355,725 residents, is the most populous <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_cities_of_Italy" title="Metropolitan cities of Italy">metropolitan city</a> in Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PR_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-PR-3">[2]</a></sup> Its <a href="/wiki/List_of_metropolitan_areas_of_Italy" title="List of metropolitan areas of Italy">metropolitan area</a> is the third-most populous within Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-citypop_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-citypop-4">[3]</a></sup> Rome is located in the central-western portion of the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Peninsula" title="Italian Peninsula">Italian Peninsula</a>, within Lazio (<a href="/wiki/Latium" title="Latium">Latium</a>), along the shores of the <a href="/wiki/Tiber" title="Tiber">Tiber</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican City</a> (the smallest country in the world)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> is an independent country inside the city boundaries of Rome, the only existing example of a country within a city. Rome is often referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Seven_hills_of_Rome" title="Seven hills of Rome">City of Seven Hills</a> due to its geographic location, and also as the "Eternal City".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> Rome is generally considered to be the "cradle of Western <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">civilization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christian_culture" title="Christian culture">Christian culture</a>", and the centre of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/History_of_Rome" title="History of Rome">Rome's history</a> spans 28 centuries. While <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman mythology</a> dates the <a href="/wiki/Founding_of_Rome" title="Founding of Rome">founding of Rome</a> at around 753 BC, the site has been inhabited for much longer, making it a major human settlement for almost three millennia and one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_oldest_continuously_inhabited_cities" title="List of oldest continuously inhabited cities">oldest continuously occupied cities</a> in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heiken,_G._2005_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heiken,_G._2005-10">[9]</a></sup> The city's early population originated from a mix of <a href="/wiki/Latins_(Italic_tribe)" title="Latins (Italic tribe)">Latins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_civilization" title="Etruscan civilization">Etruscans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sabines" title="Sabines">Sabines</a>. Eventually, the city successively became the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Kingdom" title="Roman Kingdom">Roman Kingdom</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, and is regarded by many as the first-ever Imperial city and <a href="/wiki/Metropolis" title="Metropolis">metropolis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> It was first called <i>The Eternal City</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Urbs Aeterna</i>; <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>: <i lang="it">La Città Eterna</i>) by the Roman poet <a href="/wiki/Tibullus" title="Tibullus">Tibullus</a> in the 1st century BC, and the expression was also taken up by <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Livy" title="Livy">Livy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> Rome is also <a href="/wiki/Caput_Mundi#Rome" title="Caput Mundi">called "Caput Mundi"</a> (Capital of the World). After the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">fall of the Empire in the west</a>, which marked the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, Rome slowly fell under the political control of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papacy" title="Papacy">Papacy</a>, and in the 8th century, it became the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Papal_States" title="Papal States">Papal States</a>, which lasted until 1870. Beginning with the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, almost all popes since <a href="/wiki/Pope_Nicholas_V" title="Pope Nicholas V">Nicholas V</a> (1447–1455) pursued a coherent architectural and urban programme over four hundred years, aimed at making the city the artistic and cultural centre of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> In this way, Rome became first one of the major centres of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> and then the birthplace of both the <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">Baroque</a> style and <a href="/wiki/Neoclassicism" title="Neoclassicism">Neoclassicism</a>. Famous artists, painters, sculptors, and architects made Rome the centre of their activity, creating masterpieces throughout the city. In 1871, Rome became the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy" title="Kingdom of Italy">Kingdom of Italy</a>, which, in 1946, became the Italian Republic.
</p><p>In 2019, Rome was the 14th most visited city in the world, with 8.6 million tourists, the third most visited in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, and the most popular tourist destination in Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> Its historic centre is listed by <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> as a <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whc.unesco.org_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-whc.unesco.org-17">[16]</a></sup> The host city for the <a href="/wiki/1960_Summer_Olympics" title="1960 Summer Olympics">1960 Summer Olympics</a>, Rome is also the seat of several specialised agencies of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> (FAO), the <a href="/wiki/World_Food_Programme" title="World Food Programme">World Food Programme</a> (WFP) and the <a href="/wiki/International_Fund_for_Agricultural_Development" title="International Fund for Agricultural Development">International Fund for Agricultural Development</a> (IFAD). The city also hosts the Secretariat of the Parliamentary Assembly of the <a href="/wiki/Union_for_the_Mediterranean" title="Union for the Mediterranean">Union for the Mediterranean</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> (UfM) as well as the headquarters of many international businesses, such as <a href="/wiki/Eni" title="Eni">Eni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enel" title="Enel">Enel</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TIM_(brand)" title="TIM (brand)">TIM</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_S.p.A." title="Leonardo S.p.A.">Leonardo S.p.A.</a>, and national and international banks such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unicredit" title="Unicredit">Unicredit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Banca_Nazionale_del_Lavoro" title="Banca Nazionale del Lavoro">BNL</a>. Rome's <a href="/wiki/EUR,_Rome" title="EUR, Rome">EUR</a> business district is the home of many oil industries, the pharmaceutical industry, and financial services companies. The presence of renowned international brands in the city has made Rome an important centre of fashion and design, and the <a href="/wiki/Cinecitt%C3%A0" title="Cinecittà">Cinecittà Studios</a> have been the set of many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a>–winning movies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup>
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Demographics"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Demographics</span></a>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:0142_-_Roma_-_Piazza_del_Campidoglio_-_Statua_del_Tevere_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto,_7-Apr-2008.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/0142_-_Roma_-_Piazza_del_Campidoglio_-_Statua_del_Tevere_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto%2C_7-Apr-2008.jpg/220px-0142_-_Roma_-_Piazza_del_Campidoglio_-_Statua_del_Tevere_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto%2C_7-Apr-2008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/0142_-_Roma_-_Piazza_del_Campidoglio_-_Statua_del_Tevere_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto%2C_7-Apr-2008.jpg/330px-0142_-_Roma_-_Piazza_del_Campidoglio_-_Statua_del_Tevere_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto%2C_7-Apr-2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/0142_-_Roma_-_Piazza_del_Campidoglio_-_Statua_del_Tevere_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto%2C_7-Apr-2008.jpg/440px-0142_-_Roma_-_Piazza_del_Campidoglio_-_Statua_del_Tevere_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto%2C_7-Apr-2008.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:0142_-_Roma_-_Piazza_del_Campidoglio_-_Statua_del_Tevere_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto,_7-Apr-2008.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roman representation of the god <a href="/wiki/Tiberinus_(god)" title="Tiberinus (god)">Tiberinus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Capitoline_Hill" title="Capitoline Hill">Capitoline Hill</a> in Rome</div></div></div>
<p>According to the Ancient Romans' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Founding_myth" title="Founding myth">founding myth</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livy1797_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-livy1797-20">[19]</a></sup> the name <i>Roma</i> came from the city's founder and first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rex_(king)" title="Rex (king)">king</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romulus" title="Romulus">Romulus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, it is possible that the name Romulus was actually derived from Rome itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> As early as the 4th century, there have been alternative theories proposed on the origin of the name <i>Roma</i>. Several hypotheses have been advanced focusing on its linguistic roots which however remain uncertain:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>From <i>Rumon</i> or <i>Rumen</i>, archaic name of the <a href="/wiki/Tiber" title="Tiber">Tiber</a>, which in turn is supposedly related to the Greek verb ῥέω (<i>rhéō</i>) 'to flow, stream' and the Latin verb <i>ruō</i> 'to hurry, rush';<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[b]</a></sup></li>
<li>From the <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_language" title="Etruscan language">Etruscan</a> word 𐌓𐌖𐌌𐌀 (<i>ruma</i>), whose root is <i>*rum-</i> "teat", with possible reference either to the <a href="/wiki/Founding_of_Rome#The_legend" title="Founding of Rome">totem wolf that adopted and suckled</a> the cognately named twins <a href="/wiki/Romulus_and_Remus" title="Romulus and Remus">Romulus and Remus</a>, or to the shape of the <a href="/wiki/Palatine_Hill" title="Palatine Hill">Palatine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aventine_Hill" title="Aventine Hill">Aventine Hills</a>;</li>
<li>From the Greek word ῥώμη (<i>rhṓmē</i>), which means <i>strength</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[c]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Rome" title="History of Rome">History of Rome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_city_of_Rome" title="Timeline of the city of Rome">Timeline of the city of Rome</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earliest_history">Earliest history</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Founding_of_Rome" title="Founding of Rome">Founding of Rome</a></div>
<p>While there have been discoveries of archaeological evidence of human occupation of the Rome area from approximately 14,000 years ago, the dense layer of much younger debris obscures <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palaeolithic" title="Palaeolithic">Palaeolithic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heiken,_G._2005_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Heiken,_G._2005-10">[9]</a></sup> Evidence of stone tools, pottery, and stone weapons attest to about 10,000 years of human presence. Several excavations support the view that Rome grew from <a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">pastoral</a> settlements on the <a href="/wiki/Palatine_Hill" title="Palatine Hill">Palatine Hill</a> built above the area of the future <a href="/wiki/Roman_Forum" title="Roman Forum">Roman Forum</a>. Between the end of the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> and the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>, each hill between the sea and the Capitol was topped by a village (on the Capitol Hill, a village is attested since the end of the 14th century BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coa9_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-coa9-26">[23]</a></sup> However, none of them yet had an urban quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coa9_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-coa9-26">[23]</a></sup> Nowadays, there is a wide consensus that the city developed gradually through the aggregation ("<a href="/wiki/Synoecism" title="Synoecism">synoecism</a>") of several villages around the largest one, placed above the Palatine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coa9_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-coa9-26">[23]</a></sup> This aggregation was facilitated by the increase of agricultural productivity above the <a href="/wiki/Subsistence_economy" title="Subsistence economy">subsistence level</a>, which also allowed the establishment of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secondary_sector" title="Secondary sector">secondary</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tertiary_sector" title="Tertiary sector">tertiary activities</a>. These, in turn, boosted the development of trade with the Greek colonies of southern Italy (mainly <a href="/wiki/Ischia" title="Ischia">Ischia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cumae" title="Cumae">Cumae</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coa9_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-coa9-26">[23]</a></sup> These developments, which according to archaeological evidence took place during the mid-eighth century BC, can be considered as the "birth" of the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coa9_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-coa9-26">[23]</a></sup> Despite recent excavations at the Palatine hill, the view that Rome was founded deliberately in the middle of the eighth century BC, as the legend of Romulus suggests, remains a fringe hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foundation_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-foundation-27">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Legend_of_the_founding_of_Rome">Legend of the founding of Rome</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kapitolinische_W%C3%B6lfin_Museum_Capitolini.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3935" data-file-width="5736" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Kapitolinische_W%C3%B6lfin_Museum_Capitolini.jpg/220px-Kapitolinische_W%C3%B6lfin_Museum_Capitolini.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Kapitolinische_W%C3%B6lfin_Museum_Capitolini.jpg/330px-Kapitolinische_W%C3%B6lfin_Museum_Capitolini.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Kapitolinische_W%C3%B6lfin_Museum_Capitolini.jpg/440px-Kapitolinische_W%C3%B6lfin_Museum_Capitolini.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kapitolinische_W%C3%B6lfin_Museum_Capitolini.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Capitoline_Wolf" title="Capitoline Wolf">Capitoline Wolf</a></i>, a sculpture of the <a href="/wiki/She-wolf_(Roman_mythology)" title="She-wolf (Roman mythology)">mythical she-wolf</a> suckling the infant twins <a href="/wiki/Romulus_and_Remus" title="Romulus and Remus">Romulus and Remus</a></div></div></div>
<p>Traditional stories handed down by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Romans" title="Ancient Romans">ancient Romans</a> themselves explain the earliest <a href="/wiki/History_of_Rome" title="History of Rome">history of their city</a> in terms of <a href="/wiki/Legend" title="Legend">legend</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myth" title="Myth">myth</a>. The most familiar of these myths, and perhaps the most famous of all <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman myths</a>, is the story of <a href="/wiki/Romulus_and_Remus" title="Romulus and Remus">Romulus and Remus</a>, the twins who were suckled by a <a href="/wiki/She-wolf_(Roman_mythology)" title="She-wolf (Roman mythology)">she-wolf</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livy1797_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-livy1797-20">[19]</a></sup> They decided to build a city, but after an argument, <a href="/wiki/Romulus" title="Romulus">Romulus</a> killed his brother and the city took his name. According to the Roman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annalist" title="Annalist">annalists</a>, this happened on 21 April 753 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196473_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196473-28">[25]</a></sup> This legend had to be reconciled with a dual tradition, set earlier in time, that had the <a href="/wiki/Trojan_War" title="Trojan War">Trojan refugee</a> <a href="/wiki/Aeneas" title="Aeneas">Aeneas</a> escape to Italy and found the line of Romans through his son <a href="/wiki/Ascanius" title="Ascanius">Iulus</a>, the namesake of the <a href="/wiki/Julio-Claudian_dynasty" title="Julio-Claudian dynasty">Julio-Claudian dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livy2005_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-livy2005-29">[26]</a></sup>
This was accomplished by the Roman poet <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a> in the first century BC. In addition, <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> mentions an older story, that the city was an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcadia_(ancient_region)" title="Arcadia (ancient region)">Arcadian</a> colony founded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evander_of_Pallene" title="Evander of Pallene">Evander</a>. Strabo also writes that <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Coelius_Antipater" title="Lucius Coelius Antipater">Lucius Coelius Antipater</a> believed that Rome was founded by Greeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monarchy_and_republic">Monarchy and republic</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Ancient Rome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Kingdom" title="Roman Kingdom">Roman Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a></div>
<p>After the foundation by Romulus according to a legend,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196473_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196473-28">[25]</a></sup> Rome was ruled for a period of 244 years by a <a href="/wiki/Monarchy" title="Monarchy">monarchical system</a>, initially with sovereigns of <a href="/wiki/Latins_(Italic_tribe)" title="Latins (Italic tribe)">Latin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sabines" title="Sabines">Sabine</a> origin, later by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etruscans" title="Etruscans">Etruscan</a> kings. The tradition handed down seven kings: <a href="/wiki/Romulus" title="Romulus">Romulus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Numa_Pompilius" title="Numa Pompilius">Numa Pompilius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tullus_Hostilius" title="Tullus Hostilius">Tullus Hostilius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancus_Marcius" title="Ancus Marcius">Ancus Marcius</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tarquinius_Priscus" title="Tarquinius Priscus">Tarquinius Priscus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Servius_Tullius" title="Servius Tullius">Servius Tullius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Tarquinius_Superbus" title="Lucius Tarquinius Superbus">Lucius Tarquinius Superbus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196473_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196473-28">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Palatino_(Palatine_Hill,_Rome).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1376" data-file-width="2196" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Palatino_%28Palatine_Hill%2C_Rome%29.jpg/220px-Palatino_%28Palatine_Hill%2C_Rome%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Palatino_%28Palatine_Hill%2C_Rome%29.jpg/330px-Palatino_%28Palatine_Hill%2C_Rome%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Palatino_%28Palatine_Hill%2C_Rome%29.jpg/440px-Palatino_%28Palatine_Hill%2C_Rome%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Palatino_(Palatine_Hill,_Rome).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Ancient-Imperial-Roman palaces of the Palatine, a series of palaces located in the <a href="/wiki/Palatine_Hill" title="Palatine Hill">Palatine Hill</a>, express power and wealth of emperors from Augustus until the 4th century.</div></div></div>
<p>In 509 BC, the Romans expelled the last king from their city and established an <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">oligarchic</a> <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a>. Rome then began a period characterised by internal struggles between <a href="/wiki/Patrician_(ancient_Rome)" title="Patrician (ancient Rome)">patricians</a> (aristocrats) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plebs" title="Plebs">plebeians</a> (small landowners), and by constant warfare against the populations of central Italy: Etruscans, Latins, <a href="/wiki/Volsci" title="Volsci">Volsci</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aequi" title="Aequi">Aequi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marsi" title="Marsi">Marsi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196477_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196477-32">[29]</a></sup> After becoming master of <a href="/wiki/Latium" title="Latium">Latium</a>, Rome led several wars (against the <a href="/wiki/Gauls" title="Gauls">Gauls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osci" title="Osci">Osci</a>-<a href="/wiki/Samnites" title="Samnites">Samnites</a> and the Greek colony of <a href="/wiki/Taranto" title="Taranto">Taranto</a>, allied with <a href="/wiki/Pyrrhus_of_Epirus" title="Pyrrhus of Epirus">Pyrrhus</a>, king of <a href="/wiki/Epirus" title="Epirus">Epirus</a>) whose result was the conquest of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_peninsula" title="Italian peninsula">Italian peninsula</a>, from the central area up to <a href="/wiki/Magna_Graecia" title="Magna Graecia">Magna Graecia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196479_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196479-33">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>The third and second century BC saw the establishment of Roman hegemony over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, through the three <a href="/wiki/Punic_Wars" title="Punic Wars">Punic Wars</a> (264–146 BC) fought against the city of <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> and the three <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_Wars" title="Macedonian Wars">Macedonian Wars</a> (212–168 BC) against <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedonia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196481–83_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196481–83-34">[31]</a></sup> The first <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">Roman provinces</a> were established at this time: <a href="/wiki/Sicilia_(Roman_province)" title="Sicilia (Roman province)">Sicily</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corsica_et_Sardinia" title="Corsica et Sardinia">Sardinia and Corsica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(Roman_province)" title="Macedonia (Roman province)">Macedonia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaea_(Roman_province)" title="Achaea (Roman province)">Achaea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa_(Roman_province)" title="Africa (Roman province)">Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196481–85_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196481–85-35">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>From the beginning of the 2nd century BC, power was contested between two groups of aristocrats: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optimates" title="Optimates">optimates</a>, representing the conservative part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_senate" title="Roman senate">Senate</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Populares" title="Populares">populares</a>, which relied on the help of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plebs" title="Plebs">plebs</a> (urban lower class) to gain power. In the same period, the bankruptcy of the small farmers and the establishment of large slave estates caused large-scale migration to the city. The continuous warfare led to the establishment of a professional army, which turned out to be more loyal to its generals than to the republic. Because of this, in the second half of the second century and during the first century BC there were conflicts both abroad and internally: after the failed attempt of social reform of the populares <a href="/wiki/Tiberius_Gracchus" title="Tiberius Gracchus">Tiberius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Gracchus" title="Gaius Gracchus">Gaius Gracchus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196489_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196489-36">[33]</a></sup> and the war against <a href="/wiki/Jugurtha" title="Jugurtha">Jugurtha</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196489_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196489-36">[33]</a></sup> there was <a href="/wiki/Sulla%27s_civil_war" title="Sulla's civil war">a civil war</a> from which the general <a href="/wiki/Sulla" title="Sulla">Sulla</a> emerged victorious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196489_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196489-36">[33]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Third_Servile_War" title="Third Servile War">major slave revolt</a> under <a href="/wiki/Spartacus" title="Spartacus">Spartacus</a> followed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196491_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196491-37">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196491_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196491-37">[34]</a></sup> and then the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/First_Triumvirate" title="First Triumvirate">first Triumvirate</a> with <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Caesar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Licinius_Crassus" title="Marcus Licinius Crassus">Crassus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196491_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196491-37">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trajansm%C3%A4rkte_Forum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="6000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Trajansm%C3%A4rkte_Forum.jpg/220px-Trajansm%C3%A4rkte_Forum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Trajansm%C3%A4rkte_Forum.jpg/330px-Trajansm%C3%A4rkte_Forum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Trajansm%C3%A4rkte_Forum.jpg/440px-Trajansm%C3%A4rkte_Forum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trajansm%C3%A4rkte_Forum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Imperial_fora" title="Imperial fora">Imperial fora</a> belong to a series of <i>monumental fora</i> (public squares) constructed in Rome by the emperors. Also seen in the image is <a href="/wiki/Trajan%27s_Market" title="Trajan's Market">Trajan's Market</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The conquest of <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a> made Caesar immensely powerful and popular, which led to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caesar%27s_Civil_War" title="Caesar's Civil War">second civil war</a> against the Senate and Pompey. After his victory, Caesar established himself as <a href="/wiki/Dictator_perpetuo" title="Dictator perpetuo">dictator for life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196491_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196491-37">[34]</a></sup> His assassination led to a <a href="/wiki/Second_Triumvirate" title="Second Triumvirate">second Triumvirate</a> among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Octavian" title="Octavian">Octavian</a> (Caesar's grandnephew and heir), <a href="/wiki/Mark_Antony" title="Mark Antony">Mark Antony</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aemilius_Lepidus_(triumvir)" title="Marcus Aemilius Lepidus (triumvir)">Lepidus</a>, and to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Final_War_of_the_Roman_Republic" title="Final War of the Roman Republic">another civil war</a> between Octavian and Antony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196493_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196493-38">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Empire">Empire</span></h3>
<p>In 27 BC, Octavian became <i><a href="/wiki/Princeps" title="Princeps">princeps civitatis</a></i> and took the title of <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a>, founding the <a href="/wiki/Principate" title="Principate">principate</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Diarchy" title="Diarchy">diarchy</a> between the <i>princeps</i> and the senate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196493_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196493-38">[35]</a></sup> During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Nero" title="Nero">Nero</a>, two thirds of the city was ruined after the <a href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_Rome" title="Great Fire of Rome">Great Fire of Rome</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians_in_the_Roman_Empire" title="Persecution of Christians in the Roman Empire">persecution of Christians</a> commenced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[38]</a></sup> Rome was established as a <a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a> empire, which reached its greatest expansion in the second century under the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a>. Rome was confirmed as <a href="/wiki/Caput_Mundi" title="Caput Mundi">caput Mundi</a>, i.e. the capital of the known world, an expression which had already been used in the Republican period. During its first two centuries, the empire was ruled by emperors of the <a href="/wiki/Julio-Claudian_dynasty" title="Julio-Claudian dynasty">Julio-Claudian</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196497_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196497-42">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Flavian_dynasty" title="Flavian dynasty">Flavian</a> (who also built an eponymous amphitheatre, known as the <a href="/wiki/Colosseum" title="Colosseum">Colosseum</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196497_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196497-42">[39]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antonine_dynasty" title="Antonine dynasty">Antonine</a> dynasties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196499_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196499-43">[40]</a></sup> This time was also characterised by the spread of the Christian religion, preached by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a> in <a href="/wiki/Judea" title="Judea">Judea</a> in the first half of the first century (under <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a>) and popularised by his <a href="/wiki/Apostles_in_the_New_Testament" title="Apostles in the New Testament">apostles</a> through the empire and beyond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann1964107_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann1964107-44">[41]</a></sup> The Antonine age is considered the zenith of the Empire, whose territory ranged from the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> and from <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Britain</a> to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196499_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann196499-43">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roman_Empire_Trajan_117AD.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1614" data-file-width="2534" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Roman_Empire_Trajan_117AD.png/220px-Roman_Empire_Trajan_117AD.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Roman_Empire_Trajan_117AD.png/330px-Roman_Empire_Trajan_117AD.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Roman_Empire_Trajan_117AD.png/440px-Roman_Empire_Trajan_117AD.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roman_Empire_Trajan_117AD.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Roman Empire at its greatest extent in 117 AD, approximately 6.5<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>6</sup> km<sup>2</sup> (2.5<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>6</sup> sq mi)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[42]</a></sup> of land surface</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Foro_Romano_Musei_Capitolini_Roma.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6980" data-file-width="17000" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Foro_Romano_Musei_Capitolini_Roma.jpg/280px-Foro_Romano_Musei_Capitolini_Roma.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Foro_Romano_Musei_Capitolini_Roma.jpg/420px-Foro_Romano_Musei_Capitolini_Roma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Foro_Romano_Musei_Capitolini_Roma.jpg/560px-Foro_Romano_Musei_Capitolini_Roma.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Foro_Romano_Musei_Capitolini_Roma.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Forum" title="Roman Forum">Roman Forum</a> are the remains of those buildings that during most of Ancient Rome's time represented the political, legal, religious and economic centre of the city and the neuralgic centre of all the Roman civilisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[43]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trajan%27s_Column_HD.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5456" data-file-width="2340" decoding="async" height="513" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Trajan%27s_Column_HD.jpg/220px-Trajan%27s_Column_HD.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Trajan%27s_Column_HD.jpg/330px-Trajan%27s_Column_HD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Trajan%27s_Column_HD.jpg/440px-Trajan%27s_Column_HD.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trajan%27s_Column_HD.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Trajan%27s_Column" title="Trajan's Column">Trajan's Column</a>, triumphal column and place where the relics of <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Emperor Trajan</a> are placed</div></div></div>
<p>After the end of the Severan Dynasty in 235, the Empire entered into a 50-year period known as the <a href="/wiki/Crisis_of_the_Third_Century" title="Crisis of the Third Century">Crisis of the Third Century</a> during which there were numerous putsches by generals, who sought to secure the region of the empire they were entrusted with due to the weakness of central authority in Rome. There was the so-called Gallic Empire from 260 to 274 and the revolts of Zenobia and her father from the mid-260s which sought to fend off Persian incursions. Some regions – Britain, Spain, and North Africa – were hardly affected. Instability caused economic deterioration, and there was a rapid rise in inflation as the government debased the currency in order to meet expenses. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_tribes" title="Germanic tribes">Germanic tribes</a> along the Rhine and north of the Balkans made serious, uncoordinated incursions from the 250s-280s that were more like giant raiding parties rather than attempts to settle. The <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Persian Empire</a> invaded from the east several times during the 230s to 260s but were eventually defeated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann1964101_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann1964101-47">[44]</a></sup>
Emperor <a href="/wiki/Diocletian" title="Diocletian">Diocletian</a> (284) undertook the restoration of the State. He ended the Principate and introduced the <a href="/wiki/Tetrarchy" title="Tetrarchy">Tetrarchy</a> which sought to increase state power. The most marked feature was the unprecedented intervention of the State down to the city level: whereas the State had submitted a tax demand to a city and allowed it to allocate the charges, from his reign the State did this down to the village level. In a vain attempt to control inflation, he imposed <a href="/wiki/Price_controls" title="Price controls">price controls</a> which did not last. He or Constantine regionalised the administration of the empire which fundamentally changed the way it was governed by creating regional dioceses (the consensus seems to have shifted from 297 to 313/14 as the date of creation due to the argument of <a href="/wiki/Constantin_Zuckerman" title="Constantin Zuckerman">Constantin Zuckerman</a> in 2002 "Sur la liste de Verone et la province de grande armenie, Melanges Gilber Dagron). The existence of regional fiscal units from 286 served as the model for this unprecedented innovation. The emperor quickened the process of removing military command from governors. Henceforth, civilian administration and military command would be separate. He gave governors more fiscal duties and placed them in charge of the army logistical support system as an attempt to control it by removing the support system from its control. Diocletian ruled the eastern half, residing in <a href="/wiki/Nicomedia" title="Nicomedia">Nicomedia</a>. In 296, he elevated <a href="/wiki/Maximian" title="Maximian">Maximian</a> to <i>Augustus</i> of the western half, where he ruled mostly from <a href="/wiki/Mediolanum" title="Mediolanum">Mediolanum</a> when not on the move.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann1964101_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann1964101-47">[44]</a></sup> In 292, he created two 'junior' emperors, the Caesars, one for each Augustus, Constantius for Britain, Gaul, and Spain whose seat of power was in <a href="/wiki/Trier" title="Trier">Trier</a> and Galerius in <a href="/wiki/Sirmium" title="Sirmium">Sirmium</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>. The appointment of a Caesar was not unknown: Diocletian tried to turn into a system of non-dynastic succession. Upon abdication in 305, the Caesars succeeded and they, in turn, appointed two colleagues for themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann1964101_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann1964101-47">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Abdication" title="Abdication">abdication</a> of <a href="/wiki/Diocletian" title="Diocletian">Diocletian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maximian" title="Maximian">Maximian</a> in 305 and a series of civil wars between rival claimants to imperial power, during the years 306–313, the <a href="/wiki/Tetrarchy" title="Tetrarchy">Tetrarchy</a> was abandoned. <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantine the Great</a> undertook a major reform of the bureaucracy, not by changing the structure but by rationalising the competencies of the several ministries during the years 325–330, after he defeated <a href="/wiki/Licinius" title="Licinius">Licinius</a>, emperor in the East, at the end of 324. The so-called <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Milan" title="Edict of Milan">Edict of Milan</a> of 313, actually a fragment of a letter from Licinius to the governors of the eastern provinces, granted freedom of worship to everyone, including Christians, and ordered the restoration of confiscated church properties upon petition to the newly created vicars of dioceses. He funded the building of several churches and allowed clergy to act as arbitrators in civil suits (a measure that did not outlast him but which was restored in part much later). He transformed the town of <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a> into his new residence, which, however, was not officially anything more than an imperial residence like <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a> or <a href="/wiki/Trier" title="Trier">Trier</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nicomedia" title="Nicomedia">Nicomedia</a> until given a city prefect in May 359 by <a href="/wiki/Constantius_II" title="Constantius II">Constantius II</a>; <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann1964103_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann1964103-48">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Christianity in the form of the Nicene Creed became the official religion of the empire in 380, via the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Thessalonica" title="Edict of Thessalonica">Edict of Thessalonica</a> issued in the name of three emperors – Gratian, Valentinian II, and <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a> – with Theodosius clearly the driving force behind it. He was the last emperor of a unified empire: after his death in 395, his sons, <a href="/wiki/Arcadius" title="Arcadius">Arcadius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Honorius_(emperor)" title="Honorius (emperor)">Honorius</a> divided the empire into <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">a western</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" title="Eastern Roman Empire">an eastern</a> part. The seat of government in the Western Roman Empire was transferred to <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a> in 408, but from 450 the emperors mostly resided in the capital city, Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cimeti%C3%A8re_anglais_de_Rome.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2543" data-file-width="3680" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Cimeti%C3%A8re_anglais_de_Rome.jpg/220px-Cimeti%C3%A8re_anglais_de_Rome.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Cimeti%C3%A8re_anglais_de_Rome.jpg/330px-Cimeti%C3%A8re_anglais_de_Rome.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Cimeti%C3%A8re_anglais_de_Rome.jpg/440px-Cimeti%C3%A8re_anglais_de_Rome.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cimeti%C3%A8re_anglais_de_Rome.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Pyramid_of_Cestius" title="Pyramid of Cestius">Pyramid of Cestius</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Aurelian_Walls" title="Aurelian Walls">Aurelian Walls</a></div></div></div>
<p>Rome, which had lost its central role in the administration of the empire, <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Rome_(410)" title="Sack of Rome (410)">was sacked in 410</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">Visigoths</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Alaric_I" title="Alaric I">Alaric I</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann1964115_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann1964115-50">[47]</a></sup> but very little physical damage was done, most of which were repaired. What could not be so easily replaced were portable items such as artwork in precious metals and items for domestic use (loot). The popes embellished the city with large basilicas, such as <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_Maggiore" title="Santa Maria Maggiore">Santa Maria Maggiore</a> (with the collaboration of the emperors). The population of the city had fallen from 800,000 to 450–500,000 by the time the city was sacked in 455 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genseric" title="Genseric">Genseric</a>, king of the <a href="/wiki/Vandals" title="Vandals">Vandals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann1964117_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann1964117-51">[48]</a></sup> The weak emperors of the fifth century could not stop the decay, leading to the deposition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romulus_Augustus" title="Romulus Augustus">Romulus Augustus</a> on 22 August 476, which marked the end of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a> and, for many historians, the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann1964103_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann1964103-48">[45]</a></sup> The decline of the city's population was caused by the loss of grain shipments from North Africa, from 440 onward, and the unwillingness of the senatorial class to maintain donations to support a population that was too large for the resources available. Even so, strenuous efforts were made to maintain the monumental centre, the palatine, and the largest baths, which continued to function until the Gothic siege of 537. The large baths of Constantine on the Quirinale were even repaired in 443, and the extent of the damage exaggerated and dramatised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[49]</a></sup> However, the city gave an appearance overall of shabbiness and decay because of the large abandoned areas due to population decline. The population declined to 500,000 by 452 and 100,000 by 500 AD (perhaps larger, though no certain figure can be known). After the Gothic siege of 537, the population dropped to 30,000 but had risen to 90,000 by the papacy of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_I" title="Pope Gregory I">Gregory the Great</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[50]</a></sup> The population decline coincided with the general collapse of urban life in the West in the fifth and sixth centuries, with few exceptions. Subsidized state grain distributions to the poorer members of society continued right through the sixth century and probably prevented the population from falling further.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[51]</a></sup> The figure of 450,000–500,000 is based on the amount of pork, 3,629,000 lbs. distributed to poorer Romans during five winter months at the rate of five Roman lbs per person per month, enough for 145,000 persons or 1/4 or 1/3 of the total population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[52]</a></sup> Grain distribution to 80,000 ticket holders at the same time suggests 400,000 (Augustus set the number at 200,000 or one-fifth of the population).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sack_of_Rome_by_Alaric_-_sacred_vessels_are_brought_to_a_church_for_safety_(2nd_of_2).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="543" data-file-width="729" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Sack_of_Rome_by_Alaric_-_sacred_vessels_are_brought_to_a_church_for_safety_%282nd_of_2%29.jpg/220px-Sack_of_Rome_by_Alaric_-_sacred_vessels_are_brought_to_a_church_for_safety_%282nd_of_2%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Sack_of_Rome_by_Alaric_-_sacred_vessels_are_brought_to_a_church_for_safety_%282nd_of_2%29.jpg/330px-Sack_of_Rome_by_Alaric_-_sacred_vessels_are_brought_to_a_church_for_safety_%282nd_of_2%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Sack_of_Rome_by_Alaric_-_sacred_vessels_are_brought_to_a_church_for_safety_%282nd_of_2%29.jpg/440px-Sack_of_Rome_by_Alaric_-_sacred_vessels_are_brought_to_a_church_for_safety_%282nd_of_2%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sack_of_Rome_by_Alaric_-_sacred_vessels_are_brought_to_a_church_for_safety_(2nd_of_2).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>15th-century illustration depicting the <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Rome_(410)" title="Sack of Rome (410)">Sack of Rome (410)</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">Visigothic</a> king <a href="/wiki/Alaric_I" title="Alaric I">Alaric I</a></div></div></div>
<p>After the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="Decline of the Roman Empire">fall of the Western Roman Empire</a> in 476 AD, Rome was first under the control of <a href="/wiki/Odoacer" title="Odoacer">Odoacer</a> and then became part of the <a href="/wiki/Ostrogothic_Kingdom" title="Ostrogothic Kingdom">Ostrogothic Kingdom</a> before returning to <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">East Roman</a> control after the <a href="/wiki/Gothic_War_(535%E2%80%93554)" title="Gothic War (535–554)">Gothic War</a>, which devastated the city <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Rome_(546)" title="Sack of Rome (546)">in 546</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Rome_(549%E2%80%93550)" title="Siege of Rome (549–550)">550</a>. Its population declined from more than a million in 210 AD to 500,000 in 273<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[53]</a></sup> to 35,000 after the Gothic War (535–554),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup> reducing the sprawling city to groups of inhabited buildings interspersed among large areas of ruins, vegetation, vineyards and market gardens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[55]</a></sup> It is generally thought the population of the city until 300 AD was 1 million (estimates range from 2 million to 750,000) declining to 750–800,000 in 400 AD, 450–500,000 in 450 AD and down to 80–100,000 in 500 AD (though it may have been twice this).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Bishop of Rome, called the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Pope</a>, was important since the early days of Christianity because of the martyrdom of both the apostles <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simon_Peter" title="Simon Peter">Peter</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" title="Paul of Tarsus">Paul</a> there. The Bishops of Rome were also seen (and still are seen by Catholics) as the successors of Peter, who is considered the first Bishop of Rome. The city thus became of increasing importance as the centre of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>.
</p><p>After the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lombard_invasion_of_Italy#Invasion_and_conquest_of_the_Italian_peninsula" title="Lombard invasion of Italy">Lombard invasion of Italy</a> (569–572), the city remained nominally Byzantine, but in reality, the popes pursued a policy of equilibrium between <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">the Byzantines</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Lombards" title="Lombards">Lombards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192519_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192519-60">[57]</a></sup> In 729, the Lombard king <a href="/wiki/Liutprand,_King_of_the_Lombards" title="Liutprand, King of the Lombards">Liutprand</a> donated the north Latium town of <a href="/wiki/Sutri" title="Sutri">Sutri</a> to the Church, starting its temporal power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192519_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192519-60">[57]</a></sup> In 756, <a href="/wiki/Pepin_the_Short" title="Pepin the Short">Pepin the Short</a>, after having defeated the Lombards, gave the Pope temporal jurisdiction over the Roman Duchy and the <a href="/wiki/Exarchate_of_Ravenna" title="Exarchate of Ravenna">Exarchate of Ravenna</a>, thus creating the <a href="/wiki/Papal_States" title="Papal States">Papal States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192519_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192519-60">[57]</a></sup> Since this period, three powers tried to rule the city: the pope, the nobility (together with the chiefs of militias, the judges, the Senate and the populace), and the Frankish king, as king of the Lombards, patricius, and Emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192519_60-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192519-60">[57]</a></sup> These three parties (theocratic, republican, and imperial) were a characteristic of Roman life during the entire Middle Ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192519_60-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192519-60">[57]</a></sup> On Christmas night of 800, <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> was crowned in Rome as emperor of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_III" title="Pope Leo III">Pope Leo III</a>: on that occasion, the city hosted for the first time the two powers whose struggle for control was to be a constant of the Middle Ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192519_60-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192519-60">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Detail_coronation_Charles_the_Great_(Francis_1st_of_France)_by_Pope_Leo_III_(Leo_X)_Vatican_11.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2731" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Detail_coronation_Charles_the_Great_%28Francis_1st_of_France%29_by_Pope_Leo_III_%28Leo_X%29_Vatican_11.jpg/220px-Detail_coronation_Charles_the_Great_%28Francis_1st_of_France%29_by_Pope_Leo_III_%28Leo_X%29_Vatican_11.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Detail_coronation_Charles_the_Great_%28Francis_1st_of_France%29_by_Pope_Leo_III_%28Leo_X%29_Vatican_11.jpg/330px-Detail_coronation_Charles_the_Great_%28Francis_1st_of_France%29_by_Pope_Leo_III_%28Leo_X%29_Vatican_11.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Detail_coronation_Charles_the_Great_%28Francis_1st_of_France%29_by_Pope_Leo_III_%28Leo_X%29_Vatican_11.jpg/440px-Detail_coronation_Charles_the_Great_%28Francis_1st_of_France%29_by_Pope_Leo_III_%28Leo_X%29_Vatican_11.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Detail_coronation_Charles_the_Great_(Francis_1st_of_France)_by_Pope_Leo_III_(Leo_X)_Vatican_11.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail view on an illustration by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raffaello_Sanzio_da_Urbino" title="Raffaello Sanzio da Urbino">Raphael</a> portraying the crowning of <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Saint_Peter%27s_Basilica" title="Old Saint Peter's Basilica">Old Saint Peter's Basilica</a>, on 25 December 800</div></div></div>
<p>In 846, Muslim Arabs <a href="/wiki/Arab_raid_against_Rome" title="Arab raid against Rome">unsuccessfully stormed the city's walls</a>, but managed to loot <a href="/wiki/Old_St._Peter%27s_Basilica" title="Old St. Peter's Basilica">St. Peter</a>'s and St. Paul's basilica, both outside the city wall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup> After the decay of <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Carolingian power</a>, Rome fell prey to feudal chaos: several noble families fought against the pope, the emperor, and each other. These were the times of <a href="/wiki/Theodora_(senatrix)" title="Theodora (senatrix)">Theodora</a> and her daughter <a href="/wiki/Marozia" title="Marozia">Marozia</a>, concubines and mothers of several popes, and of <a href="/wiki/Crescentius_the_Younger" title="Crescentius the Younger">Crescentius</a>, a powerful feudal lord, who fought against the Emperors <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otto_II" title="Otto II">Otto II</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otto_III" title="Otto III">Otto III</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192520_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192520-62">[59]</a></sup> The scandals of this period forced the papacy to reform itself: the election of the pope was reserved to the cardinals, and reform of the clergy was attempted. The driving force behind this renewal was the monk <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ildebrando_da_Soana" title="Ildebrando da Soana">Ildebrando da Soana</a>, who once elected pope under the name of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_VII" title="Pope Gregory VII">Gregory VII</a> became involved into the <a href="/wiki/Investiture_Controversy" title="Investiture Controversy">Investiture Controversy</a> against Emperor <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry IV</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192520_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192520-62">[59]</a></sup> Subsequently, Rome <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Rome_(1084)" title="Sack of Rome (1084)">was sacked and burned</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Normans</a> under <a href="/wiki/Robert_Guiscard" title="Robert Guiscard">Robert Guiscard</a> who had entered the city in support of the Pope, then besieged in <a href="/wiki/Castel_Sant%27Angelo" title="Castel Sant'Angelo">Castel Sant'Angelo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192520_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192520-62">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>During this period, the city was autonomously ruled by a <i>senatore</i> or <i>patrizio</i>. In the 12th century, this administration, like other European cities, evolved into the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_commune" title="Medieval commune">commune</a>, a new form of social organisation controlled by the new wealthy classes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192520_62-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192520-62">[59]</a></sup> Pope <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucius_II" title="Lucius II">Lucius II</a> fought against the Roman commune, and the struggle was continued by his successor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pope_Eugenius_III" title="Pope Eugenius III">Pope Eugenius III</a>: by this stage, the commune, allied with the aristocracy, was supported by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arnaldo_da_Brescia" title="Arnaldo da Brescia">Arnaldo da Brescia</a>, a monk who was a religious and social reformer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192521_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192521-63">[60]</a></sup> After the pope's death, Arnaldo was taken prisoner by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adrianus_IV" title="Adrianus IV">Adrianus IV</a>, which marked the end of the commune's autonomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192521_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192521-63">[60]</a></sup> Under <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_III" title="Pope Innocent III">Pope Innocent III</a>, whose reign marked the apogee of the papacy, the commune liquidated the senate, and replaced it with a <i>Senatore</i>, who was subject to the pope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192521_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192521-63">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>In this period, the papacy played a role of secular importance in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>, often acting as arbitrators between Christian <a href="/wiki/Monarch" title="Monarch">monarchs</a> and exercising additional political powers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faus_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Faus-64">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Papal_Arbitration_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Papal_Arbitration-65">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1266, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_of_Anjou" title="Charles of Anjou">Charles of Anjou</a>, who was heading south to fight the <a href="/wiki/Hohenstaufen" title="Hohenstaufen">Hohenstaufen</a> on behalf of the pope, was appointed Senator. Charles founded the <a href="/wiki/Sapienza_University_of_Rome" title="Sapienza University of Rome">Sapienza</a>, the university of Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192521_63-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192521-63">[60]</a></sup> In that period the pope died, and the cardinals, summoned in <a href="/wiki/Viterbo" title="Viterbo">Viterbo</a>, could not agree on his successor. This angered the people of the city, who then unroofed the building where they met and imprisoned them until they had nominated the new pope; this marked the birth of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conclave" title="Conclave">conclave</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192521_63-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192521-63">[60]</a></sup> In this period the city was also shattered by continuous fights between the aristocratic families: <a href="/wiki/Annibaldi_family" title="Annibaldi family">Annibaldi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caetani" title="Caetani">Caetani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colonna_family" title="Colonna family">Colonna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orsini_family" title="Orsini family">Orsini</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conti_di_Segni" title="Conti di Segni">Conti</a>, nested in their fortresses built above ancient Roman edifices, fought each other to control the papacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192521_63-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192521-63">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pope_Boniface_VIII" title="Pope Boniface VIII">Pope Boniface VIII</a>, born Caetani, was the last pope to fight for the church's <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Universal_domain&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Universal domain (page does not exist)">universal domain</a>; he proclaimed a crusade against the <a href="/wiki/Colonna_family" title="Colonna family">Colonna family</a> and, in 1300, called for the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jubilee_(Christianity)" title="Jubilee (Christianity)">Jubilee of Christianity</a>, which brought millions of <a href="/wiki/Christian_pilgrimage" title="Christian pilgrimage">pilgrims</a> to Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192521_63-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192521-63">[60]</a></sup> However, his hopes were crushed by the French king <a href="/wiki/Philip_IV_of_France" title="Philip IV of France">Philip the Fair</a>, who took him prisoner and killed him in <a href="/wiki/Anagni" title="Anagni">Anagni</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192521_63-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192521-63">[60]</a></sup> Afterwards, a new pope faithful to the French was elected, and the papacy was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avignon_papacy" title="Avignon papacy">briefly relocated</a> to <a href="/wiki/Avignon" title="Avignon">Avignon</a> (1309–1377).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192522_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192522-67">[64]</a></sup> During this period Rome was neglected, until a plebeian man, <a href="/wiki/Cola_di_Rienzo" title="Cola di Rienzo">Cola di Rienzo</a>, came to power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192522_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192522-67">[64]</a></sup> An idealist and a lover of ancient Rome, Cola dreamed about a rebirth of the Roman Empire: after assuming power with the title of <i><a href="/wiki/Tribune" title="Tribune">Tribuno</a></i>, his reforms were rejected by the populace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192522_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192522-67">[64]</a></sup> Forced to flee, Cola returned as part of the entourage of Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Gil_%C3%81lvarez_Carrillo_de_Albornoz" title="Gil Álvarez Carrillo de Albornoz">Albornoz</a>, who was charged with restoring the Church's power in Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192522_67-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192522-67">[64]</a></sup> Back in power for a short time, Cola was soon lynched by the populace, and Albornoz took possession of the city. In 1377, Rome became the seat of the papacy again under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gregory_XI" title="Gregory XI">Gregory XI</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192522_67-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192522-67">[64]</a></sup> The return of the pope to Rome in that year unleashed the <a href="/wiki/Western_Schism" title="Western Schism">Western Schism</a> (1377–1418), and for the next forty years, the city was affected by the divisions which rocked the Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192522_67-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192522-67">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_history">Early modern history</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Roman_Renaissance" title="Roman Renaissance">Roman Renaissance</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wolf-Dietrich-Klebeband_St%C3%A4dtebilder_G_123_III.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1503" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Wolf-Dietrich-Klebeband_St%C3%A4dtebilder_G_123_III.jpg/220px-Wolf-Dietrich-Klebeband_St%C3%A4dtebilder_G_123_III.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Wolf-Dietrich-Klebeband_St%C3%A4dtebilder_G_123_III.jpg/330px-Wolf-Dietrich-Klebeband_St%C3%A4dtebilder_G_123_III.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Wolf-Dietrich-Klebeband_St%C3%A4dtebilder_G_123_III.jpg/440px-Wolf-Dietrich-Klebeband_St%C3%A4dtebilder_G_123_III.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wolf-Dietrich-Klebeband_St%C3%A4dtebilder_G_123_III.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Almost 500 years old, this map of Rome by <a href="/wiki/Mario_Cartaro" title="Mario Cartaro">Mario Cartaro</a> (from 1575) shows the city's primary monuments.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:0_Castel_et_pont_Sant%27Angelo_(1).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2568" data-file-width="3852" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/0_Castel_et_pont_Sant%27Angelo_%281%29.JPG/220px-0_Castel_et_pont_Sant%27Angelo_%281%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/0_Castel_et_pont_Sant%27Angelo_%281%29.JPG/330px-0_Castel_et_pont_Sant%27Angelo_%281%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/0_Castel_et_pont_Sant%27Angelo_%281%29.JPG/440px-0_Castel_et_pont_Sant%27Angelo_%281%29.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:0_Castel_et_pont_Sant%27Angelo_(1).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Castel_Sant%27Angelo" title="Castel Sant'Angelo">Castel Sant'Angelo</a>, or Hadrian's Mausoleum, is a Roman monument radically altered in the Middle Ages and the Renaissance built in 134 AD and crowned with 16th and 17th-century statues.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fontana_della_Barcaccia_restaurata,_guardando_verso_Piazza_Mignanelli.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Fontana_della_Barcaccia_restaurata%2C_guardando_verso_Piazza_Mignanelli.jpg/220px-Fontana_della_Barcaccia_restaurata%2C_guardando_verso_Piazza_Mignanelli.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Fontana_della_Barcaccia_restaurata%2C_guardando_verso_Piazza_Mignanelli.jpg/330px-Fontana_della_Barcaccia_restaurata%2C_guardando_verso_Piazza_Mignanelli.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Fontana_della_Barcaccia_restaurata%2C_guardando_verso_Piazza_Mignanelli.jpg/440px-Fontana_della_Barcaccia_restaurata%2C_guardando_verso_Piazza_Mignanelli.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fontana_della_Barcaccia_restaurata,_guardando_verso_Piazza_Mignanelli.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Fontana_della_Barcaccia" title="Fontana della Barcaccia">Fontana della Barcaccia</a> by <a href="/wiki/Gian_Lorenzo_Bernini" title="Gian Lorenzo Bernini">Gian Lorenzo Bernini</a> in 1629</div></div></div>
<p>In 1418, the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Constance" title="Council of Constance">Council of Constance</a> settled the <a href="/wiki/Western_Schism" title="Western Schism">Western Schism</a>, and a Roman pope, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martin_V" title="Martin V">Martin V</a>, was elected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192522_67-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192522-67">[64]</a></sup>
This brought to Rome a century of internal peace, which marked the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192522_67-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192522-67">[64]</a></sup> The ruling popes until the first half of the 16th century, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicholas_V" title="Nicholas V">Nicholas V</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Vatican_Library" title="Vatican Library">Vatican Library</a>, to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pius_II" title="Pius II">Pius II</a>, humanist and literate, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sixtus_IV" title="Sixtus IV">Sixtus IV</a>, a warrior pope, to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_VI" title="Alexander VI">Alexander VI</a>, immoral and <a href="/wiki/Nepotism" title="Nepotism">nepotist</a>, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julius_II" title="Julius II">Julius II</a>, soldier and patron, to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leo_X" title="Leo X">Leo X</a>, who gave his name to this period ("the century of Leo X"), all devoted their energy to the greatness and the beauty of the Eternal City and to the patronage of the arts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192522_67-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBertarelli192522-67">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>During those years, the centre of the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a> moved to Rome from Florence. Majestic works, as the new <a href="/wiki/St._Peter%27s_Basilica" title="St. Peter's Basilica">Saint Peter's Basilica</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sistine_Chapel" title="Sistine Chapel">Sistine Chapel</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ponte_Sisto" title="Ponte Sisto">Ponte Sisto</a></i> (the first bridge to be built across the <a href="/wiki/Tiber" title="Tiber">Tiber</a> since antiquity, although on Roman foundations) were created. To accomplish that, the Popes engaged the best artists of the time, including <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pietro_Perugino" title="Pietro Perugino">Perugino</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>, <a href="/wiki/Domenico_Ghirlandaio" title="Domenico Ghirlandaio">Ghirlandaio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luca_Signorelli" title="Luca Signorelli">Luca Signorelli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sandro_Botticelli" title="Sandro Botticelli">Botticelli</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cosimo_Rosselli" title="Cosimo Rosselli">Cosimo Rosselli</a>.
</p><p>The period was also infamous for papal corruption, with many Popes fathering children, and engaging in <a href="/wiki/Nepotism" title="Nepotism">nepotism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Simony" title="Simony">simony</a>. The corruption of the Popes and the huge expenses for their building projects led, in part, to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Reformation</a> and, in turn, the <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a>. Under extravagant and rich popes, Rome was transformed into a centre of art, poetry, music, literature, education and culture. Rome became able to compete with other major European cities of the time in terms of wealth, grandeur, the arts, learning and architecture.
